RATING: 8
I have limited experience but, here are my thoughts so far;

Seems a little lighter than weighted after you 'catch-on' - more like a 2.5 maybe? Fun and replayable. Good-looking too. Balanced with a good mix of choices, blocking tactics, area take-over tactics with a definite beginning, middle and end with changing strategy throughout. Suffers from a little AP. Experienced players could probably beat the 120 minute time listed.

My copy seems to have been published by Ravensburger.

RATING: 7
This game deservingly won all of the major game awards (SdJ, DSP, IGA).  It combines an outstanding theme, highly innovative mechanic (action points), has very good components, and has excellent scalability (works exceptionally well with 2, 3, or 4 players).  You even have the option of making it lighter/more luck driven (random, blind flipping of tiles) or MUCH more strategic (bidding for tiles, the only method I ever use).  This is a must-own for any serious game collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tikal is a very interesting game, and beautiful as well. I think Mexica edges this one out a bit due to it's more fluid nature. Tikal felt more choppy in play. I'll keep playing this one (but not late at night) because it is a very good game. I don't think it'll become a great game though, at least not for me. UPDATE: I recommend that each player draws their next tile to lay at the end of their turn, rather than the beginning of their next turn. That way they have an entire round to consider where they will place their tile. This is similar to house rules for Carcassonne, and I believe it greatly speeds up the game.

RATING: 7
1 play / 4 players (2h)

LIKE: well designed with a lot of tension. I like the intermediate scoring, which adds a lot of interaction for temple control.
DISLIKE: the non-auction version of play is too luck dependent. the game can get a little cutthroat with some blocking and runaway leader.
OVERALL: fun, tactical game with good ideas. It's interesting how the scoring works where multiple players can score a temple in a row, and opt to build on to it. I like how as the jungle is explored, the better temple tiles come out, which fits the theme well. Many moving inter-connected parts. The auction variant and other two-player variants are a must.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In the Tikal board game you are the director of an expedition negotiating the myriad paths through the jungle. Your expedition leader and his team following your every step. On your journey you discover temples long lost from the memory of humankind. These temples are excavated and the treasure troves nearby unearthed. During the four scoring rounds of the game you are rewarded handsomely with victory points for the treasures you have and for the temples that you control. The expedition that wins the most points is the winner.

RATING: 7
Hrm.  I like the AP system, the actions.  I enjoy the tile placement, the choice of treasure/temple pursuit, how to set up to do well in scoring.  The theme's nice, I like exploring the ancient ruins, jungle feel, etc.

The actual volcano-based scoring seems to give a random advantage to someone and feels weird and inelegant.  Also, most importantly, the game is [b]LONG[/b] for what it is.  We'll try with one of the "shrink it down" variants, and might play occasionally with that.  Without, I don't think I feel like taking the time.

UPDATE:  Ok, with instant-scoring to make it shorter + less forgiving of poor worker placement, and removal of nine tiles it becomes a 30-40 minute game with a good bit of tension, interesting decisions, and a nice board.  Not a favorite, but quite enjoyable!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2-4(4)

It's a fine game. The AP is there, but I don't think it's necessarily a huge problem. Keeping track of the action points was sort of a bigger deal. And then that everything stops for the scorings is another matter.

I like that you can take a bunch of small, incremental actions to establish yourself, but then you need the big expensive actions in order to really secure your position (set up a camp, guard a temple, uncover a temple, grab a treasure) and so it's a matter of trying to time things so that you can get those actions at the best time (e.g. guarding a temple without losing half your researchers).

It looks beautiful and it's nice the way that the terrain sort of organically emerges.

RATING: 10
Excellent game, both to play, and to look at. Good action point movement system, and a chance for some wicked opportunism. There's no room for polite play, as you do whatever is neccesary to discover and improve temples, unearth treasures, and do your damndest to pinch temples from the other players, who are all trying to do the same to you. Someones gonna end up shafted!
I went looking for this game after it had good reviews, and I wasn't dissapointed. It helps that I often win!
On the strength of this, I went looking for Mexica. With the possible exeption of 'Civilization' or 'Settlers of Nurembourg', THIS is the best game that I own.

RATING: 7
I've now played this classic twice in the past month after owning it for over 10 years without a single play.  It uses a variation of the standard area control mechanic.  Both plays used the bidding mechanic, which usually gives me a advantage as I'm typically very good at assessing value and almost never over-bid.  Without it, however, might render the game too random since the ability to select the right tile can make or break your game.  The game itself is very beautiful, and while fairly abstract, the theme is very well integrated.

RATING: 9
A "solve the puzzle" feeling to this game with a very pleasing explore the jungle theme.  The scoring mechanic, wherein each person moves his pieces in the scoring round then scores only his position feels weird, original and ultimately interesting, very much underscoring the puzzle versus competition duality that is inherent in this classic. It is a puzzle (solve it!)...no, it is a competition (compete!). It's both.

RATING: 7.5
Interesting mix of resource management and competitive strategy. Scoring happens every few rounds, so  you need to plan ahead by building up the board, temples and gathering treasures to maximise points whilst all the while working around your competitors who are doing the same. 

When scoring round arrives temples quickly change hands in order to maximise the points gathered and make it more challenging for the opponnent to do so.

RATING: 10

The game can be a little dependent on the luck factor with 2-player games. **My copy of this game didn't have the directions and so when I finally got the directions I found out I had been playing this game incorrectly. These are the 3 differences : When a volcano tile is drawn each player scores. Without the before and after actions. The 2nd difference was unlimited temple guards. 3rd we didn't take explorers out of the game if on the same tile when a temple guard was being placed.** Now that I have the directions I still play the way I used to without the rules listed above.

RATING: 7
Tikal. Player are expedition crews exploring the ruins of the Mayan city of Tikal, trying to uncover Temple floors and collect sets of treasures. On their turn, player have ten action point, which they spend to various things. Points are score in four stages, three during the game and one at end.

I generally liked it. It's fairly easy to learn and it does have a lot strategy in play. My biggest complaint is its action point system, which while does drive a lot of the action, can be hard to keep track of at times.

RATING: 7
Tikal is one of the most visualy appealing games I own.  It's one of the few games in my collection that has both strategic depth and is thematicaly very strong.  It's very satisfying to watch the jungle start to clear away as you explore the game board --  To see the temples begin to emerge from the map as more and more people uncover them -- and to watch the regions fill up with more and more explorers running around searching for treasure and glory.

It's true that the game can be a tad slow between turns and players can be stuck for long periods of time trying to work out their best possible move.  But these weaknesses are more than made up for by the overall design and presentation.

RATING: 9
What a beautiful game! I'm almost tempted to get this game simply for the artwork. WOW. Game play is exciting, if somewhat similar to Kramer's other action point system games. Still have to try the auction variant which will probably make the rating go up.

Update (2006-11-29): I've still yet to play the auction vairant but I'm starting to find the basic version a bit frustrating due to the tile draw. Dropped my rating down to 7 from 8.

Update (2006-12-19): Finally played the auction variant and this is definitely the way to play the game. You get what you pay for and you get some added tactical elements due to the ability to bid for turn order. Turns a 7-8 rated game to and 8-9 rated game, for me. Will refrain from playing the basic version, unless playing with newbies.

RATING: 7
Már régóta érett, hogy kipróbáljam ezt a (mondhatjuk?) klasszikust. Nem bántam meg, tetszett! A rövid és designos szabályfüzet, meg a 10+ alapján családi játékra számítottam, de szerintem ez inkább gémer játék. Nem túl komplex, de azért agyalós. A szabály egyébként nagyon egzakt, (a falualapításnál szerintem kicsit hibás, de lehet hogy csak a magyarban írták el). A játék elsőre rendkívül kiegyensúlyozottnak tűnt! Az első 3 értékelésnél max 3 pont különbség volt, és nem mindig ugyanaz vezetett. A végén ment csak 10 fölé a difi. A kincseknél és lapkáknál van szerepe a szerencsének, de kincses játéknál ez belefér. Nagyon igényes a kivitelezés is, összességében megérte kipróbálni, máskor is játszanék vele, sőt a 2. rész is érdekelne:)
---
Majd ki akarjuk próbálni a haladót, mert a teljesen random lapkahúzogatás azért elég igazságtalan tud lenni. Ha vulkánt húz valaki, az inkább szívás, ha kincses lapkát, az inkább jó, de azért helyzetfüggő is a dolog.
---
Tényleg jobb a haladó, de a kezdőt is szívesen játszom, csak az úgy családibb. Kipróbáltuk időközben a mini tikal játékváltozatot is. Lemegy 25 perc alatt, de az előkészület és elpakolás is legalább annyi. Nekem kicsit talál túl rövid is így a parti.

RATING: 7.5
Shame on me for having waited to try this classic so long. It is a very straightforward action points game, with a small dose of luck (although I hear it is mitigated in the auction variant), and (thanks to the gorgeous components) a very thematic feel.
What is amazing is the elegance of the design, that gives the game an almost gateway feel (still it is surprising a gamer's game like this won SdJ a while ago!).
The only problem I see is the potential downtime between turns, as players prone to analysis paralysis take their actions. Anyway, it is certainly one of the best action points games around, plays wonderfully even with 2, and has the most useful box insert I ever saw in a boardgame!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]Spiel des Jahres 1999[/b]
[b]Deutscher Spiele Preis 1999[/b]

[i]Posted: 9/20/05, Rated: 7[/i]
{2P: 7, 3P: 7, 4P: ???}

Since I am a new inductee to "Euro" games, I am always interested to discover a mechanic in a game that is new to me.  With [i]Tikal,[/i] that mechanic is found in the way that the tiles are shuffled together.  This is the first time that I have seen such a method for adding some structure to a random deck.

I am still learning when it comes to tile placement: sometimes I want the jungle to be very fluid, connecting to as many different spaces as possible so that I can take advantage of my opponents' activities.  At other times, I want to make movement very costly, or even block off a portion of the jungle altogether so that my opponents can't benefit from my own efforts.  These thoughts can seriously slow down tile placement.

There are four scoring opportunities during the game.  When one occurs, each player gets ten "action points" to move her units around the table, capturing treasures or uncovering temples to maximize her score.  This process really grinds the game to a halt, as each player attempts to find the optimal actions that score the most points.

Tikal is a very enjoyable game with a great theme.  It also offers occasional turns of crushing analysis paralysis (AP), and for this reason I do not think it has been as well received by my family as [i][gameid=9209][/i].
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I sought this game out simply because it was a Spiel des Jahres winner, even though I didn't have the highest expectations, but I was surprised how much I enjoyed it. This is actually a really neat game. I was a little nervous about how the action point system might lead to analysis-paralysis with the wrong players, but it wasn't too bad. Since so many of the actions (especially later in the game) chomp up large chunks of your action points, players end up only taking a few actions on their turn. I like that there are multiple strategies to explore, and every turn you have to make a tough decision about which actions will benefit you the most. The exploration theme is sweet and it is actually very unique compared to other games in my collection. I've only played the basic game so far, but I'm not sure that the advanced game is necessary, it seems good enough as it is. My one complaint would be that the game seems just a little long. I think if we get a few games under our belts it will go quicker, though.

RATING: 9
Games typically run close to 2 hours, but as is said of good games the time is well spent.  A lot of directions to contemplate for the 10 actions that are allowed. Very tactical, even with the auction version, but at the same time need a strategic outlook with regard to placement of camps in the field of play. Might need to play the auction version to even out the tile draws with experienced players, but I still think the game plays well without it. It is very important to place one's camps in a strategic place on the grid to optimize worker placement. This is a gamer's game, and offers repeated plays due to the unknown placement of tiles for each round.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Tikal is one of those pillars of Euro design, in this case, the specific contribution being action points.  As far as area control games go (that aren't specifically 2 player), this is one of the best.  There is certainly too much to do and not enough actions, which generates quite a bit of tension.  I enjoy the action point system here more than in the other games that have employed it - I found Torres to be too abstract, while Cavum didn't work so well with 2.  Tikal does it all right, but that said, it does feel a little rusty.  A true classic, but one that will only come out intermittently.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Another excellent Kramer-Kiesling "action-allocation" abstract. The game is much more thematic than their other area control abstracts (Bison: Thunder on the Prairie, for example), and the rules are incredibly intuitive and easy to teach. The pure-information and totally deterministic movement/actions make this still not my favorite type of game, especially as you can sometimes determine the winner a couple of turns before the end of the game, but it does make the game tight and intensely competitive, which I like. Deserving of its "classic" status.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
While I think Tikal was a decent game, it did show sign of its age, and while I would play it again I don’t need to own it. I think it’s an unforgiving game for new players, who will have a hard time playing well in this game; at least that was what happened in my first game. We were two new players against an experienced one, and looking back we made a ton of bad decisions (but I was actually close to winning; I still don’t understand how)! It is not wise to have a lot of people standing in remote areas, and the importance of a new camp around the middle became quite obvious after our first play… 

What was good about Tikal was that it was a rather fast and didn’t have nearly as much AP as I had heard. The best part of the game was probably the “3” person as that guy can really mess up your opponents plans! The ability to lock a temple was quite neat too as was the spatial element; while it wasn’t the main part of the game, clever placement of the drawn tiles is essential to win. But this was perhaps one of the major downsides of the game; the luck of the draw. 

Yes, I have played with the non-auction variant both times as we didn’t know the game and knew which tiles would come up and what the value of them were etc. In my next games I am definitely going to play with the auction variant, as the tiles you randomly draw can vary a lot in terms of usefulness and give one player a big advantage (if he constantly draws good temple tiles while somebody else constantly draws blank tiles). In addition, it is random when scoring occurs (you only know approximately when), and this can give a player an advantage if his placement right before the scoring is good; yes, I know, you all get 10 action points to spend right before scoring which reduces this fact, but still).

 Finally, I didn’t like the rule about how you can trade a treasure chip with another player in order to complete a set, but the other player cannot trade the other way… Yes, I do realize why this rule is there, so you don’t swap back and forth, but I still think that yet a lucky draw of two similar treasures early in the game gives you an advantage, especially if somebody else draws the last treasure of that kind. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Rated 8 after one play. I know this has a reputation for analysis-paralysis, but the first play with my fellow responsible, conscientious players went very smoothly and shorter than I expected. I really like this game and the flexibility that 10 action points provide.

On the one hand, it's a very abstract game. But I actualy enjoy the theme and feel that the theme really reinforces the mechanics. It's neat seeing the jungle unfold as your "dudes" spread out to explore all the temples. Maybe it's because the components are so good. This is a visually impressive game, especially for a game published prior to 2000. I certainly look forward to more plays.

Update: after my "nickel" play, I'm bumping this up to a 9. Every time I play this, I really enjoy it. I've yet to be disappointed by this game, and right now, I can't imagine this ever leaving my collection.

RATING: 8.5
I like Tikal (not Tikal II).  I got it from the HK war game club with HK$215 during big sales period.  I like it very much.  I can classify this game between family and gamers game.  It is an old classic action point game.  Best with 2 and 3 people.  The game may have some downtime problem because each player should take 10 action points for each round.  10 Action points?!! ...you see 10 action points can generate so much downtime.  It's the only draw back of this game but I think the designer is brave to make this decision and rules on this game.  I can see the confident and elegant design of the designers.  This game is in small luck but high tactics. 
The components are good with wooden pieces.  The art design is cool.  The game can carry the theme well.  The action is smooth with a good player aid.  Easy to learn even for non-gamers (10 minutes can be used to explain the rules).  Normally I'll play this game without using the auction rules.  I like playing game with simple and elegant flow.

This game is suitable playing with non-gamers.  However, the leading person should be patient enough.  Avoid playing this game if you are analysis paralysis killer!!!

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Auction Rules. Another home run for Wolfgang Kramer, Tikal has everything I love in a board game, auctions, tight decisions, and pulls it all together with wonderful production values. The balance between treasure mongering and temple influence is spot on, and the differing ways to develop the board should give it excellent longevity. Hideous opportunism abounds in this game, and knowing when to bid high on the volcano tile, when to throw a few more barrels in the forest, when and how much to excavate a given site, and most importantly, when to sacrifice your grad students to the fiery god in trade for repeated guaranteed scoring of temples all are tricky decisions. These difficult options are the backbone of this game, as it is these nuances which will make or break your score by the final round.

RATING: 8
Tikal is a beautiful game to look at and play with, and features an excellent subject supported by intruiging mechanisms based on action points, or AP for short. Wolfgang Kramer has made many games in which AP play a role, and this is his best. The players are explorers in the jungles of Central America and are searching for overgrown temples. The higher these temples, or the more excavated they are, the more points they are worth; and more points mean a higher chance at achieving victory. All available actions have a certain fixed 'cost' associated with them, and within a small handful of boundary rules the player is free to chose which actions he will take... Until his 'credit' for his turn runs out. A lovely mechanism as it rewards player creativity.

Tikal scales well with any number of players, but has a slightly annoying tendency to cause analysis paralysis in inexperienced players. Some people enjoy to calculate their way out of this game; I prefer to play by heuristics and a little intuition, using the action points only as a sort of bookkeeping aid. I don't believe it is necessary to become a human computer either: usually the board position is such that it is fairly obvious how your main plan should work out for this and the next few turns. The random tile element does not please some people, I find it adds a little schwung to the game which fits the exploration theme very, very well. I tried playing the game with the auction variant once, and me and my partners broke off halfway through. What is a nice and pleasing rythmical game becomes a grating and calculative mess. There [i]is[/i] more to this game than blatant min-maxing: it's also about not painting yourself into a corner, and that is not as easy as it looks.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
Ugh, never again.  There's just so much that's so situational- am I going to be the one who has to spend the three AP on forcing a trade that's beneficial to both players because of the way the turn order falls on a scoring round? Am I going to draw a blatantly worse tile than the previous person?  I can't control any of it, so why does this take ninety fucking minutes?

RATING: 10
Bought this new for $34.95 US at Hobbytown USA in Wisconsin.  (Boy, that was a great game store!  I bought a lot of good games at that one stop.)
This is one of my very favourite games.  I do well at it.  2-4 players.  I love the tile discovery aspect, so every game is different.  Sometimes we play with the auction variant, but I slightly prefer just taking turns in order, making the best of what you flip over.  It's an area control game.
This was the first game I bought designed by Kiesling and Kramer.  I later bought Tikal II (not as good), and Torres (very good, but I have only played it a few times.)
Fantastic game components.  Even a fantastic box insert!  It is one of the few Rio Grande Games that I own.
It is one of three games by the same designers in the Mask Trilogy.  I have not played the others (Java, in 2000, and Mexica, in 2002), but I keep my eyes open for them in game stores.  I understand that they all use the same action point system.  Torres is considered the unofficial fourth game in that trilogy, and I agree.
I love this game!

I bought a second copy for $14 from Connol Radke's garage sale.  The rule book is just photocopied though.

RATING: 8
Overall, an outstanding game from Kramer and Kiesling where action points combine wonderfully with area control and tile placement. Easy to teach, perfect middle-weight game, prone to a bit of AP for some players, with enough elements of luck in the tile-draw version to keep the game fun and exciting, and everyone on their toes. The auction version ups the AP a bit, but also takes away some of the luck for a more strategic game. A delightfully fun game to play with a nice pace and rhythm that rewards player creativity and is just amazing in terms of simple yet deep elegance. One of my favourites.

RATING: 7
I really enjoy the overall design of the game, especially the archaeology aspect. A good balance of luck and skill. RATING: 7
One of the prettiest games out there. The board is a gorgeous shade of green, and as the game is played it becomes sprinkled with three dimensional pyramids and volcano tiles that make it even nicer. The stones mechanism for movement not only works well, it seems to create the feel of moving up and down stairs in the jungle and gives the board even more dimension. The game play is good as well, but in the basic game the random draw of tiles can win or lose the game--if the same person always draws the volcanoes he is unlikely to win. There is an advanced way to play that involves auctioning the tiles, but I have never played that way, as I understand it also lengthens the game considerably.

Update: A recent play revealed a big flaw to me: there is no catch up mechanism in the game. If someone jumps out to a lead there is really no way to catch him.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
- Gameplay and replayability: 3/3. Superb positional strategy and high replayability, reminding me vaguely of Chess.

- Luck factor: 3/3. There is some luck involved in drawing random tiles, but it is very small can be compensated for. And if that still bothers you, there is a (slightly less elegant) rule variant that rules out the luck factor completely.

- Player range: 3/3. Tikal is very good with any number of players, also with 2.

- Duration: 2/3. The game takes approximately 1.5 hour, independent from the number of players. Analysis paralysis must be countered by coughs and humming, otherwise it will ruin the game.

- Learning curve: 2/3. Tikal can be explained in some 10 minutes, which is great in my opinion. It takes some experience to be able to win however, which is also a good thing.

- Theme and looks: 2/3. Magnificent game board and a plausible although a bit shallow theme.

- Set up/clean up time: 3/3. Setting up the game takes some 5 minutes, cleanup is even faster.

- Documentation: 3/3. Clear and unambiguous rule book and good quick reference documentation.

- Summary: This is the best board game I've played so far.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Incontournable ! Il est impensable de ne pas posséder un exemplaire de Tikal dans sa ludothèque.
Le thème est fort, très fort. L'immersion est totale dans une ambiance de trésors perdus et de temples enfouis dans une forêt tropicale. Les mystères de l'exploration sont parfaitement bien rendus grâce à un principe de tuiles hexagonales que l'on pioche au hasard à son tour et que l'on pose en laissant apparaître un nouvel endroit de la forêt.
Le matériel est absolument somptueux, les règles simples et très claires s'apprennent en 15 minutes. A chaque partie, le terrain sera différent puisqu'il est construit au fur et à mesure de la partie. Les rebondissements de situation, les courses aux trésors et la lutte pour le contrôle des temples rendent les parties tendues du début à la fin.
On peut y jouer en ligne : http://www.spielbyweb.com/

RATING: 7
This is an solid game which I quite enjoy.  It's one of those games where you are given so many "action points" per turn and you have a lot of choices how to use them.  As such, it is light on player interaction, and heavy on max/min'ing.  This means that sometimes it can run a bit long for a euro...especially w/players that take forever trying to find the optimal usage of the action points.  Admittedly, I had to force myself to make (most likely) sub-optimal moves a couple of times because I didn't want to draw out my turn.  Our games have run a bit over 2 hours.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Beautiful looking game, themed on the excavation of an ancient South American city. The board is emerald green jungle to begin with, but gradually opens up as large hex tiles fill in the board. Tiles contain temples (which can be excavated further to increase their value), treasure locations (which can be looted for points), empty areas or volcanoes (time for a scoring round). Players spend 10 action points each turn to bring their diggers onto the board and attempt to win influence over the temples and treasures. Points are scored for treasure sets and majorities in temple areas. A serious 90 minute game that spawned several other &quot;spend action point&quot; games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
When the BBG was first created it was I who put "Action Point Allowance System" into the database, much to Derk's chagrin.  Tikal was probably the preminent game I had in mind when I put this awkwardly worded game mechanism category in there.

I love this kind of game mechanic, it makes any game, regardless of theme, feel like a wargame to me.  The point system gives some feel of production of units for your armies, while at the same time giving a feel for "command and control" aspects of waging war.  As a high ranking commander there are only so many things you can focus on in the grand scheme of things and the point system allows enough flexibility to decide how much you want to focus on one particular aspect of the conflict.

Of course all of this language has nothing to do with Tikal in terms of its theme, it's about uncovering ruins in the jungle, but all of those elements are very much present if you look past the direct theme.

I really like Tikal.  It has this mechanic that I really enjoy using in a game, the game itself is gorgeous and wonderfully symbolic in explaining the rules on the components.  The action points wed very well to the theme so things don't feel all that pasted on for a euro game, it's all good stuff.

The problem has more to do with time and other players for myself.  The game is on the longer side and can suffer from analysis paralysis if players are wont to do that type of thing.  You also really ought to have everyone playing at a similar level with one another for more challenging play.  

Because of those issues this is more of a classic that should be revisted now and again rather than something that gets picked up all the time.  I don't think I'd ever discard with my copy, but I don't see myself clamoring to pull it out at every game session.

RATING: 5
This is an interesting area control, tile laying and action point game. If you're playing this game, I would highly recommend using the auction variant if you want to reduce the randomness. The trade off is that, when playing the auction variant, expect your game to last longer.  I enjoy the tile laying which in a way relates to route building because when you lay tiles you're determining how people access various tiles and how many action points it will cost to get to that tile. There are also some actions that are limited in the game such as laying tents (which helps with movement) and guarding temples (which means only you will gain VP for that temple). Overall this is a good game, but it can easily lead to analysis paralysis and tends to make the game the game overstay it's welcome.  Even without AP, I find the game to be too long for the enjoyment I get out of it.

[b]Rating based on - 25+ plays, 2 player[/b]
I love the theme of this game, I love the exploration and area control elements, and we also really like the auction even though we do not like auctions or bidding in games. Increasingly though, I feel that Seeland has replaced this game for me. While I have a good time when I play, something just seems a bit lacking for me. It has now sat on the shelf unplayed for almost a year.

I keep coming across comments about how brilliant this game is, and how agonising it is not to be able to have enough action points to do everything...In our games it's not uncommon to hear "I'll do this, this and then this, and then....hmmmm....I'm good." (with 7 action points left!) We tried the mini Tikal variant but we found it too short and quite unsatisfying so I don't know how to 'fix' this game for us.

RATING: 7
Great looking game but feels like a math puzzle.  I like to play games from the gut and do my evaluations in a spatial way instead of making math based decisions.  I don't think the action point mechanic is for me since it makes me figure out each move in my head several way to make sure I'm doing the optimal move.  All the decisions and over think do not add up to more fun.    I enjoy it if I can keep it on a light family level.  On scoring rounds I reach for a calculator...  Only Kramer would make you tally lots of small numbers into scores in the 80's.  I don't want to work that hard for a Eurogame.  I'm not worried about the AP factor but the counting is tedious.  Having said that, I'm close to rating this game an 8 because I really do like it and the production is fantastic.

RATING: 6
[Action points, Tile-laying, Area majority, Exploration, Auction] [4P:5, 3P:6, 2P:6] Beautiful board, nice bits, cool theme. One of the "Action Point" games from Kramer and Kiesling, Tikal is interesting and thought-provoking. However, I can see how some groups get paralyzed by analysis in the game, especially in the late stages. Well, ok, deciding what tiles to bid on can also take some time. A "menu" of options is rarely a good thing for speedy play, and when the other players are idle, then it leads to downtime. Good game, but only for groups who can make decisions without crunching too many numbers. *** UPDATE: The Auction variant is definitely the only good way to play this game, as the random tile draws influence the game too much, but it draws the game out too much. The one-auction-per-tile thing is painful. Is there a way to do one auction for all four tiles? I suppose, but that's not in the rules. Knocked down from 7 to 6. It's not as good as Java or Mexica - its siblings have advantages over it (Java is better overall, at the same speed; Mexica is faster and cleaner). ***UPDATE: Unless invited to play by someone else, while I'll probably do, there's never any reason to pick Tikal over Torres, Java or Mexica. It's just not as good, and not as fun, as the other three games. As an additional penalty, it takes twice the time to play compared to Torres and Mexica, about as long as Java, which is a big minus as it doesn't have the depth to warrant that kind of time investment. Finally knocked down to its final score of 5. ***UPDATE: Ok, back up to 6. I'll play if asked, as long as everyone can play at a speedy pace. Tikal is decent, though it's still my least-favored game of the Mask Trilogy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Great majority and placement game. You really feel like clearing the jungle, you see the pyramids rising up, you collect treasures ...

The tension is there throughout the game: the revelation of the four tiles volcano (count points) is a sword of Damocles and makes it necessary to optimize your travel and hold well the game to score effectively.

However, subject to analysis paralysis that can dramatically slow the flow of the game.

The game board and materials are wonderful, so that one wants to return soon.

EDIT : I prefer it to his brother Tikal II that is "too much of all" (teleportation, rondel...) and more abstract. I won't buy Tikal II, Tikal is definitely purer.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Rules Complexity: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Strategic Depth: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:
Luck Factor :halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Negotiation Factor: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Amount of Downtime: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Fiddliness: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::star:
Player Interaction: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:

I'm a big fan of this game.  I think that for most of the game, downtime is not too much of an issue, contrary to public opinion, at least when playing with average players. There are a lot of choices, but if you make a few longer term plans and you’re not blocked at every turn, it doesn’t have to take long to simply follow through with them. The last scoring round can drag if it’s a close game.

Pros: 
- It's a very pretty game. The artwork is very detailed (down to tire treads imprinted on the dirt beside the base camp) and lush.
- The idea of exploring a jungle is usually pretty appealing. People that like Carcassonne have a good chance of wanting to try this.
- The rules are relatively simple. In fact, for a game with this amount of strategic depth, I think it has some of the simplest rules out there.
- It doesn't take any longer to play with 4 people than 3.

Cons:
- It can easily take a couple of hours to play.
- With people that have to make just the right move every turn, downtime can be an issue.
- A newbie doesn't have much chance of beating experienced players (I see this as a pro, but many newbies won't).

RATING: 8
Tikal needed a bit of time and a few plays to grow on me, but I like it very much now. It's a very direct and to-the-point game with a fairly mean streak and quite a bit ahead of its time. Area majorities where several players can claim the same majority still seem like a stroke of genius to me.

10 action points for turn make for a lot of options, but I haven't experienced the AP issues so many people seem to be concerned about. Besides, it's beautiful. I haven't actually played the auction variant yet (except in the two-player mini variant), but I don't think it is necessary either.

RATING: 8
I do enjoy Kiesling & Kramer's Action Point system. Tikal is the second of his 'Action Point' games - with Torres being the first. Torres is quite different with its 3Dness, but Tikal is quick, easy-to-play fun with the right amount of depth and beauty. I think the theme is excellent and Tikal is a classic example of Kiesling & Kramer's AP mechanic.  The simplest of their 'Mask' games to play, but also the most thematically rigorousness one as well.  The others can play quite abstractly at time (regardless of theme).  Though Tikal is simpler, it hits all the right mechanics-to-theme buttons.

RATING: 6
Great artwork and quality construction. Nice theme. The timing of scoring rounds is randomized, so one must be careful to maintain good board coverage throughout most of the game so as to avoid missing an opportunity to score.

Tikal does not rate higher for me because I think it plays too long for what it is.  Furthermore, the action point system can be problematic for anyone who is prone to analysis paralysis--the downtime between turns can easily become excessive, especially in a 4p game.  (I have played a 2p game of Tigris and Euphrates with the player on my right during the Tikal downtime.  This greatly improved the experience!)

RATING: 7.5
I have come to see Tikal differently over time.  Recently, I have tired of the standard rules and the inherent randomness of the tile draw.  Also, in standard mode only 2 and 4 player games have any real balance.  In 3 player games it has been my experience that the players that "mix it up" the most will tend to hand the victory to the 3rd player. My rating for the standard rules is therefore "5" but must be averaged with the "7" I give the auction variant of Tikal.

My good game buddy Ed found one of the last Ravensburger editions on line because I was so needing Tikal at the time.

RATING: 8
Super Meeple production is top notch!  Love the resin temples like in Mexica.

Tile placement, excavating, movement, guarding, treasure swap.. all are quality decisions and scale nicely at 2.

Mini Tikal not necessary as we played the full game quite quickly, less than 90 mins.  Didn't seem to have longer turns at the end, in fact the opposite.

Has an open feel but immediately after your first move you set your team in motion and start committing. 

Random scoring times also keeps the tension high but since you get a turn beforehand, keeps the aggravation and luck low.



Negatives:
The archealogist meeples are too small for my tastes.  Probably to allow for contested spaces in a 4 player game?  A slightly larger stackable version would have been preferred.

Rulebook should be multilingual since English and German speakers will also want this nicer version.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This is a fun little game. It seems a bit complicated when you're trying to hash it out at the beginning, but once you start playing, it's actually a pretty simple game that rewards forward-thinking, aggressive players. Folks with good math-crunching brains ought to do well at this, because most of it involves figuring out how you can wring the most points out of a possible scoring turn. You are rewarded by playing each round as if the volcano were about to appear and making sure that your pieces on the board have good potential to earn you some points when your turn comes 'round. It's an easy game to get into and also quite easy to enjoy. I wanted the game to last a little longer, though, but part of playing Tikal is wanting that one more turn to get another level on that temple before you guard it. Good, clean family fun.

RATING: 6
Tikal is a game which I respect but don't really enjoy. The analysis woes you hear about everywhere are somewhat well-founded, as the possibilities are so many and varied each turn. You really can spend lots of time going through the possible iterations in your head, just to get that ideal, perfectly efficient turn. The result is that the game can really drag. However, it has to be said that this is a deep and subtle game, and that the analysis is well rewarded if you're into that kind of thing and have like-minded opponents. Action point games are not for me, but this is certainly one of the best of its kind, and I can see why it's regarded as a classic.

RATING: 9.7
One of the best area influence game that i have ever played. The action point system works well i love it. However because you are not really involved in the other players turns this can cause big downtime. It is more likely to happen in the end of the game when analysis paralises becomes a true factor. On the other hand this game is easy on rules and super easy to teach. I mean with other words it is elegant. Of course it is long around 2 hours, but it grants very low luck factor and played like chess. For example a situation you might encounter in the game: If i put this here she will go there, so i will put there instead. Hopefully the game grants very high level of player interaction, where you are constantly try to kick out somebody from the temples by fighting for them. It is not rare that one of your enemy built up a great value temple and you are just sneaking some dudes into it and steal his work :). If someone playing this in a clever way it is hard to stop stomeone building up a bunker. I believe with the right backround music this is a very thematic exploration game.

Pros / Cons:
-Every game is different, which means huge replayability.
-High intreaction area influence with some element of screw your neighbour.
-With the bidding rules it has low to no luck factor.
-Also really thematic exploration game.

-The gameplay is 2 hours, with huge downtime near the end.
-Prone to analyzis paralizis i suggest you to not play this game with these type of people.
-Too hard to stop someone when he started to build up his bunker.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
2-8-10
I like this game a lot.  It is easy enough to see how to get points yet deep enough to make you think a lot about how you want to go about getting them.  There are a lot of good decisions to make each turn while simultaneously pondering where your opponents may go.  It has that abstract level of conflict that I think adds to the game and really shines.  The way scoring works is nice as everyone gets an opportunity to get a go for maximum points.  The artwork is great and I really like the modular board.

The only downsides are that it is quite AP-prone, making me fear that the better you get at the game, the longer it would take as you see more and more options to analyze with greater knowledge and strategy.  Also, the groupthink kingmaking can turn into 'he's getting x this turn, she needs y, I will take points from him instead'.  It's a blessing and a curse - good to easily see who will be ahead but also curse because people can really try to screw up your area if they want.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I bought this game solely on the awards it has won, and after playing it, I understand. It is a "classic" Euro type game. It's prone to analysis-paralysis with all those action points, but it is a fun game with a rich, deep theme. I really feel like I am exploring the jungles of South America, uncovering treasures and excavating pyramids. The basic game seems a touch unbalanced, due to a deterministic ending, but this is fixed with the optional auction rules. A fun game which represents what present-day board gaming is all about. A must have in any collection. Rating "9".

Update (07/24/2009): Managed to get this one out finally after being neglected for over a year. I only play the auction rules now, and also teach it first too. 15 minutes to explain, 2.25 hrs for a 4P game with two newbies and two veterans, so the 90 min play time is pretty sporty for first-timers. Still a great game--simple mechanics, yet very deep play, and satisfying board development.

Update (01/07/2011): Played the basic rules to accommodate a newbie; never again! How the tiles fall is deterministic--whomever is the first player will score first in the final scoring round; whomever is last, will score last, suffering a significant disadvantage. The rules regarding the last scoring round is clearly broken. Suggest that the turn order be lowest score gets to move and score first, similar to the Power Grid "worst goes first" mechanism. Otherwise, basic rules is a '6'.

Update (11/17/2014): Managed on 3 played in five years? Yes, the Analysis Paralysis could be aggravating. Yes, there's a problem with the scoring using the basic rules. I think it is a important and significant design, and I still want it in my collection, but my, my dear Tikal, you are showing your age with the action point mechanism. I don't know when I will have the urge to play this again. Dropping my rating down to "7".

RATING: 7
[i]First game of the AP Trilogy.[/i]

This game changes dramatically with each added player—2, 3 & 4p games are all radically different in feel.  Best to play with a minute-timer or pre-agreed turn time limits—each turn has a mathematically optimal move which can take some people decades to find.  Better to force intuition with some form of timer.  Or public humiliation.

TWELVE YEARS LATER

For the love of God get 10 cubes or beads (if you're reading this it's a good bet you can simply stand up and walk straight to such a thing) so people can count down their AP (used here to mean "action points", not "analysis paralysis"—though they're easily confused, I know) to make the accounting more precise, especially during asterisk-inducing takesies-backsies.

With 2, it plays quite fast but there is usually not enough interaction. Three players seems to be the best spot for Tikal.  Good player interaction, reasonably short time (but still too long for my taste).

RATING: 6.4
Finally got a chance to play this classic.  This is one euro-game that has a very strong theme with it.  It's fun to watch the jungle unfold and see your little meeples scamper across the landscape.

And of course there's the "Action-Point-Allowance System".  Yes, it allows for individual play styles.  Yes, it could drag game play down to a snail's pace.  I haven't played it with any AP  players, but I shudder at the possibilities.

In the end, my only big flaw with this game was the lack of ways to score points.  It's true you can each take your own paths, but each player is still just going to be claiming temples and treasures.  The temple fighting was at least entertaining, but it did lead to some difficult decision making when combined with action points.

RATING: 6
One of the most exciting Action Point mechanic games. Comparing with other AP games, Tikal has more simple rules and concrete concepts to achieve victory. For this reason, the game progresses favorably and smoothly. Most of other AP games are horrible for long time thinking. Unlike other AP games, Tikal can slightly release players from pondering. Of course, this game also has some kind of limitations such as other AP games. Though, Tikal is still attractive in simple rule details and easy to make a plan. This is the best choice for someone who has apprehension on AP games. For me, Tikal is the best AP game.

RATING: 8
Exploration:Area influence:Action point movement:Set collection:Auction(variant)

An excellent strategy game with an awesome theme and very little luck. Initial rating 2 plays but don't expect it to move much. This is a very well put together game with concise rules that are easy to learn and it comes with very high quality components, some of the best I have seen, even the insert in the box is PERFECT.

I think it will be best with 3 but it is still very good with only 2 players. 2 players is spread out with a 'little' less confrontation and 4 would be very interactive with fighting over everything. Not as prone to AP as some would say, potential is there but most times turns are taken quite quickly with only 2 at least. Auction rules spoil the exploration a little but make for more strategic play, so far I don't find it necessary, a little luck/randomness is welcomed to keep it interesting. I can see how it would make a bigger difference in a 3 or especially a 4 player game. We will be trying it just for variety sake. Love this game so far.

[u]UPDATE[/u]: Rating dropped a little after teaching a newbie the game. It was still a good game but longgg, 3 player has that much more AP vs. 2 player which had only a little, 4 must be bad and I wouldn't want to try it. The auction variant worked nicely with 3 and there were some good fights over temples. I think I prefer the 2 player game of this now though just due to it being too long for what it is with 3, more plays will tell. 

[u]UPDATE[/u]: 8.5 => 8 Dropped again, I only want to play this with 2 players and at the scale it is excellent, I could be convinced to play with 3 but won't suggest it (Tikal=2, Java=3, Mexica=4)

RATING: 8
Soort meerderhedenspel in de jungle. Elke speler heeft een team expeditieleden met een expeditieleider. In je beurt leg je een nieuwe zeshoekige tegel (met open plek, tempel, schatten of vulkaan), waardoor een stuk oerwoud ontgonnen wordt, en spendeer je max. tien actiepunten (expeditieleden op het bord brengen, schatten delven of ruilen, tempels ontdekken of bewaken, verplaatsen, of een kamp bouwen). Telkens een vulkaan tevoorschijn komt, volgt een scoreronde met punten per tempel waar je de meerderheid hebt, en voor schatten die je verzameld hebt. Tof spel, maar schatten leveren systematisch een groot voordeel op, waardoor punten (toch bij twee spelers) sterk uiteenlopend kunnen zijn. 

Spel 2015: 25 EUR 2e h, Sinterklaas 2015 voor mij.

RATING: 6
Reasonably short eurogame.  You're competing teams of archeologists exploring the jungle and getting points for excavating temples and collecting artifacts.  The game is pretty simple with some interesting maneuvering as you place the tiles and try to establish control over temples while grabbing other people's temples that they have invested effort into excavating.  I enjoy the games in a faint sort of way.  There isn't a lot of richness to the strategies and there is a certain amount of screwing each other that is not my favorite thing.  The theme is reasonably well developed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tikal is an enjoyable take on area control that gives players multiple options and forces them to solve the dilemma of whether to work hard and improve the value of their controlled areas or simply stepping in and take the credit for new discoveries.

It has two major issues: the randomness of the tile draw and a tendency to run way longer than it should (especially due to new players) thanks to complicated moves. There is an auction variant in the manual that is strongly recommended - it fixes the first problem and improves the second by allowing players to start formulating plans while others are taking turns.

2015-09-11

Tikal is a good game. Nowadays I prefer to play with the variant auction but it adds lots of downtime so I only do it if all players are fast players and they know the rules, otherwise we play regular Tikal which is also a blast.

In this form, there is some luck of the draw with the tiles, but I have found that the game still have lots of depth, so the most skillful player will win most often.  I’m downgrading its rating so it reflects my feelings for the game without any variant.

Tikal has a special place in my heart, it was the first game I played that used action points, so for me, it was very novel. Still, after all this time and after knowing many more games, I continue to come back to Tikal from time to time. I really enjoy it.

It features easy rules but very complex decisions (which is a problem with AP prone people).

It has beautiful art and when you finish the game, it looks gorgeous on the table. 

Current Rating: 7.0

I have at least 5 plays before 2010 thus I don´t have logged them.

RATING: 8
You get ten actions points on a turn. Turn up a tile, place it where it extends the exploration region the best, and use your action points to move your workers, dig on temple and treasure sites, and establish a new base camp. Most workers on a dig site gets you the points, and digging makes it worth more.

Burn my brains. Caused me to 'fess up and make and buy an Analysis Paralysis microbadge.

Many options each turn. And you can plan for a few turns ahead. Decisions decisions decisions!

Extremely nice to look at too! There's a couple of minor things that grate against the theme.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A classic with surprising gateway appeal to many gamers. Great theme, gorgeous colors and components, easy to teach. You may have heard loose talk of downtime and analysis paralysis in this game, but I've yet to see it. Not a game I'd want to play every week, but every 2-4 would be perfect. Players have 10 action points to spend every turn on a variety of options, and the board never looks the same different tile placement produces all kinds of crazy pathways to navigate. That is by far the most interesting part of this game: it's a jungle out there, and figuring out how to best move around (and where other players are unlikely to follow) is a blast. 

Tikal is great casual fun, but (like lots of games) can be somewhat frustrating when expert and novice players are at the same table. The questions of "where, when, who, and to what extent" are tougher than they first appear. Probably the least obvious thing about this game is how aggressively the table needs to compensate against early or midgame leaders. There's an auction variant to remove the randomness (making such compensation explicitly required instead of implicitly necessary), but the intense brain-burn is too high a price to pay - I'd rather the players had a simple awareness of the randomness and what it means in practical terms. 

Recommended with four players.  

Tikal is the kind of game that makes me wonder if I need to go to a half-point rating system. 8.5 would be about right. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players have ten action points each turn. Tiles are placed that have markings indicating the number of action points to pass through a side. Players move workers over the tiles to pyramids which they can then uncover to increase the value. Your workers may have to compete with other players to have control of a pyramid. Some tiles contain treasures and pairs and triplets give additional points. When a volcano comes out you have one more set of actions and then scoring occurs for the pyramids and treasures. I have played the board game and iPad versions and like both.

RATING: 9
Game is played in sequence with each player laying down an adjacent hex tile - which depedning on how it's placed will allow you to explore the jungle.  This gameplay makes the map random everytime! Tiles are shuffled and players play first tile in the stack, then players have 10 points to maneuver. With these 10 points per turn, you are able to gain treasue, and temples, and explore the jungle.  The the game can change completely just from another another players move, strategy is very tough!

RATING: 9
Loads of tension and player interaction. Limited action points leave you with though choices and turns that are challenging to optimize. 
The enjoyable decision points in this game are many:
-where and when to place out your tent(s)
-how strongly to fight for treasures
-how much to invest in a struggle to control a temple
-where to deploy your 'super' worker
-when to use the temple defence ability
-where to place map tiles

The rules are fairly straightforward but the game play is rich and dynamic. It's area majority at it's very best. 

There are few majority games as intense and exciting as this one, it's almost too good to have won the SdJ award! Seriously, it's that good.

Winner of SdJ 1999

I was initially very impressed with this 1999 Spiel des Jahres winner --- much more depth and complexity than I have come to expect from that award, and an excellent feeling of exploration mirrored by the beautiful artwork serves to create a much more unified theme than in the majority of German games.

UPDATE: Having played it a few more times, I still feel like the payoff is not quite enough.  It's definitely heavier than other SdJ winners, and the theme/artwork is evocative.  But I hate the sort of boring count-out-how-many steps you can take out of 10.  Worker placement seems like all the rage these days (and this game did it earlier and better), but it's still a mechanic that seems too much like being an accountant.  I want to be Indiana Jones in the jungle, not the dude who sends 8 men exploring by radio!

My rating has settled for now at a 6.

RATING: 8

It's as though a 'curse' takes over and your scientists transform into old Mayan gods or rulers. Odd things in theme like that tend to bug me.

RATING: N/A
Tikal is a game of exploration within the Central American jungles in search of lost temples and the treasures within. Players send their team of explorers into the jungle, exposing more and more of the terrain. Along the way, you find temples that require further uncovering and treasures. Players attempt to score points for occupying temples and holding onto treasure.

RATING: 9
Kramer & Kiesling!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.4
Owned since Dec 2010 – played once or twice every month or 2.

[b]Rating Scale[/b]
1.0 components / design
1.0 mechanics 
0.9 Learnable / Teachable
1.0 re-playability / Frequency
0.8 Time to Satisfaction ratio
1.0 Game Ending Satisfaction
0.9 Captivating / Down Time
0.8 Social Interaction
1.0 Group Enjoyment
1.0 Personal Enjoyment

Total Score 9.4

[b]Thoughts[/b]
The scoring phases are great to tally and secure points.  It is unique in this aspect, that one temple may render you points on a scoring phase, then another player can take over and score on the same temple.  

I highly enjoy the energy points to spend with each turn.  It allows you to manage and calculate best strategy.  

As the map unfolds there is some control as to how to isolate high scoring areas for yourself by making them monumentally difficult to travel too.  But, you can possible counter that too.  

It is well worth the 1 1/2 hours to play.

RATING: 7.5
[2007.Jan.3] One of the "Mask Trilogy" action point games by veteran designers Kiesling & Kramer; the others being Java and Mexica. Not quite as heavy as Java is, this game is slightly more prone to analysis paralysis (for those who tend to suffer from this terrible gamer's affliction). A beautifully rendered romp through the jungles of Central America, what more could an Indiana Jones wannabe want? Gather up your jacket, hat, revolver, and whip you're gonna need 'em!

RATING: 10
Azt hiszem ez a kedvenc játékom. Szeretem benne, hogy egyrészt szép, bele lehet élni magam a témába, másrészt pedig hogy nehéz. 10 cselekvési pont. Sok is és kevés is. Megtehetem ezt is, azt is, meg amazt is, de ezek közül csak egyet vagy kettőt, és nehéz kiválasztani, hogy mire költsem a 10 pontot. Nagyon jól eltalált, élvezetes játék. Lehet benne gonoszkodni (ellopni a másik kincseit, elfoglalni a feltárt piramisait, vulkánkitörést intézni a tábora szomszédságában, stb.), de lehet tök békésen, konfrontáció nélkül is játszani. Én optimálisan próbálok játszani, ami elég nagy agymunkát igényel, de hát épp ezt élvezem benne... És hogy nem a szerencsén, hanem tisztán a taktikámon és az ellenfelek taktikáján múlik, hogy ki kerül ki győztesen, hisz a játékosok ugyanabban a dzsungelben ugyanolyan lehetőségekkel játszanak (főleg a profi változatban).

A játék 2, 3 és 4 játékossal is teljesen jól működik, ugyanolyan stílusú marad, a holtidő (amíg a többi játékos lép) nem unalmas (hiszen érdekes a többiek cselekvéseit követni), és a játéktér sem rendeződik át annyira, hogy az zavaró lenne akár egy picikét is. Most csak egyetlen a játékosszámtól függő eltérést érzek: több játékosnál nagyobb lehet az igény a Profi játékra, amikor a terepkártyákat nem csak véletlenszerűen húzzuk, hanem licitálunk értük. (De ezt még nem próbáltam.)

Update: A játékostársam legutóbb egész kellemetlenül játszott, ráadásul sikeresen: egy 10 szintes piramisomat két értékelésnél is megkaparintotta. Így, hogy - bár nem teljesen szándékosan - agresszívan játszott, sokkal nehezebb volt a játék, kihívás. Élveztem nagyon. 9 -> 10 pont.

RATING: 6
I'm a bit disappointed. People are complaining about down-time and they are right. Although Tikal is a territory control game at its heart, which would imply lots of interaction, you actually wait patiently for your turn and then decide what to do - there's no other strategy anyway since the board is constantly changing and your opponents can make very different moves from what you've expected. I've spent around half the time OFF the table (I was playing at a local gaming club, so I could watch other games) and completely ignored what the others were doing and finished second anyway - I could have won - not if I were watching the game more closely, but if I hadn't wasted my time fighting over one temple and concentrated on gathering more treasures instead... In one word: boring. Although definitely not a bad game - just not my cup of tea.

RATING: 7
This is a fine and beautiful game with a lot to think about.  Managing your workers is a challenging experience.  MY only beef is that it tends to take a little long to play (and that becomes worse with players who want to take a long time over each turn).  As a result, we have never played the auction version which certainly LOOKS interesting, but appears impractical.

As an update and addendum, after playing the 4 player game with random draws, it is a lot more possible with more players for someone to get a poor run of draws (or, conversely, a very good set), skewing the game to the lucky player, which seems wrong for a game of this type.  The auction would alleviate that, so is probably the way to go for a full 4 player game.  With 2, each player sees half the tiles, making the unlucky draw much less of a factor.  There the extra length does not give the better game.

RATING: 10
We love this game. It has a wonderful balance between luck and strategy. The area control mechanism is fun and challenging - using the tablets between tiles to help "fend off" competitors so that you can guard palaces with the fewest men. At times the treasure seems like the key to the game - get the most and you will win - but as soon as you try to rely on that, someone will win handily without much treasure. Tikal plays great with two, three, or four players. WIth two it is more controlled, as you can better understand your opponent's strategy. With four it is more chaotic, as you have folks coming at you from all directions. Overall, we enjoy the game more every time we play it. At least for the time being, we think it is one of the best.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
2005 Comments:

I remember back in 1999 when Rio Grande Games was still a small fish in a big pond of game manufacturers'. Jay had had a few well recieved games in the previous 2 years with the likes of Samurai, Bohnanza, Kahuna, Mississippi Queen, Canyon and Lowenherz (which are all very good), but in 1999 his list of published games tripled and Rio Grande started to earn the stellar reputation he now has in the gaming world. In those days, you could buy just about ANY RG game and know that you were going to have a good time. Tikal was definately one of those games.

I was very fortunate to hear that Jay was going to be at Match Play in Mountain View during the weekly Wednesday nite game session and I had already heard a little about Tikal at that time on rec.games.board (BGG was not even around at this time). I went to game night and was thrilled to meet Jay and I got to talk with him at length about his games and even bent his ear about my  own game I was designing at the time (and still am!).

When it was time to play, I mentioned that I really was interested in trying Tikal and Jay obliged and pulled out his brand spanking new copy of it. He went through the rules in a few minutes and got us up and running as he watched the first few turns to make sure we were ok before teaching another game to the next group. 

Tikal was amazing. I had never seen anything like it. The board and artwork were simply stunning, even to this day I would be hard pressed to mention another game with such beautiful components. What really impressed me however, is how involved the game was. EVERY turn was agonizing as you dont have enough action points to do everything that you want to do. There is a brilliant balance of methods to earn victory points and the scoring rounds themselves were unique in that all players took a turn "during" another players turn.

Jay came up at one point when one of the guys was taking a long time to take a move and simply said "This is a fast paced German game". We had no more problems after that and I've used that line every time I've taught the game and our group doesnt suffer from the curse of Analysis Paralysis like others seem to have with Tikal.

The other thing that I love about this is the THEME which is elegantly woven into the gameplay. You really feel like you are Indianna Jones and you are searching for the Ark of the Covenant. I can't see this game using any other setting and still being as big of a success as it is. I will never turn down a game of Tikal.

...[b] Abstract [/b]...__________[]...[b]Themed [/b].....
.........[b] Luck [/b]..._________[]_...[b] Skill [/b]...........
......[b] Simple [/b]..._______[]___...[b] Complex [/b]...
..[b] Strategic [/b]..._______[]___...[b] Tactical [/b]....
....[b] Friendly [/b]..._______[]___...[b] Cutthroat [/b]..
.......[b] Fiddly [/b]...__________[]...[b] Elegant [/b].....

RATING: 8
Rating based on 3 & 4, using standard rules.

I really, really see why AP-prone players should stay away from this game (Tikal has a rep), but as long as you play with people who play from the gut (at least in the early going) it's a good & fun game with simple rules, lots of freedom, and some thinky bits. It's kind of like a competitive Pandemic, where most of the game revolves around the logistics of getting from one place to another using a set number of action points. It's quite satisfying to pull of an efficient combo of moves and gain yourself a slew of points. 

I think the main thing about this game is to realize that there is a fair amount of luck involved in the tile draw, so play this in a relaxed way and you'll have more fun. If you worry to much about playing perfectly, not only will you get bogged down with AP but you'll get more upset by the feeling it's not balanced. (I would NOT want to play with the auction variant, btw, which solves the luck problem but would dial the AP-proneness to the max)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Well-designed, thoughtful game has players exploring the jungle.  The big problem some have with this one if that each turn players receive 10 action points to spend that can bog down into analysis paralysis.  I haven't had this issue as I've only played on SBW, which seems to be a perfect fit for such a game.  The gameplay is challenging as each player is vying for a limited number of points which are earned by both collecting treasure and having the majority at a temple when the board is scored.  My favourite mechanic is that each player takes turns scoring: which means that even though you have a majority at a temple you may want to add more explorers to it to keep others from scoring it on their round.  Additionally, you may want to place an explorer on a temple that you won't score (because of a tie, etc.) to force others to use their valuable action points in order to score it on their turn.  Their really are a ton of ways to spend your action points and ultimately it's a contest in efficiency.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a great game that I wish I could play more often.  I probably wouldn't have even known about it if it hadn't won the Spiel des Jahres.

in Tikal players are archeologists digging in the jungle for treasures and temples.  Each turn the player takes a new hex piece to add to the board as the discovered area.  These pieces will either be empty, or more commonly they will have some buried treasure or a temple.  After placing the piece the player has 10 action points to spend.  These can be spent by bringing out new people, moving them, digging temples (making them worth more points during scoring), digging up treasures, building a tent (used to give you access further away from the start point as the board gets larger), or claim a temple outright for yourself.  Mixed throughout the hexes are volcanoes, these represent scoring rounds.  Whoever draws the volcano gets to first take 10 action points to position themselves to score.  You score based on treasures you have, and temples you have the majority at.  After scoring the next player gets their 10 actions to score with, and so on.  In this manner it is possible for all players to score at the same temple, after the first player takes majority and scores, the second player could then take over majority and score it and so on.

This is a great game with a lot of options.  Most of the time you don't have a clear option as to what your best move is.  Because of this AP prone players may cause the game to slow down a bit.  This was a great SDJ choice and I love it when I get a chance to play it.

[b]Game Length:[/b] (Short) :star::star::star::nostar::nostar: (Long)

RATING: 7
Played once with 4, no auction.  Very pretty game.  Quick turns and lots of fun min-max-mathy fun.  We didn't use a single guard in our game, which I'm assuming was poor play, but that's how it worked out.  We dug up treasures very quickly so half way through the game they were all gone.  Not sure if that was good or bad.  I'm assuming the auction-variant would be a lot more complex, though I'm not sure I'd want to bid VP's on tiles most of the time?  I enjoyed it.  It was neat how the scoring round worked so that everyone had a chance to score after each of their turns, that helped to prevent anyone from really getting screwed which was kinda nifty.  You can kind of use a general strategy most of the time, and then really concentrate on scoring rounds to squeeze the most points out that you can.  It really helps the game flow.

[size=10][u][i][b]r{Initial Thoughts}r (after 5 plays):[/u][/b][/i][/size]
* Classic action point Euro with a treasure-hunting theme (part of the [i]Mask Trilogy[/i])
* Plethora of actions at one's disposal can lead to severe AP (analysis paralysis)
* Players walk a fine line between discovering/plundering and restocking workers
* Several rounds of scoring, but falling behind early is usually insurmountable
* Gameplay is somewhat thematic, as the board is "discovered" as the game progresses
* Good for 2 (more tends to drag) and those who enjoy the action point mechanic
* Initial rating: [b]8/10[/b]

RATING: 9
A great area control type of game where players control a group of workers exploring the Jungles of Tikal and unearthing temples and treasure to score points. I thought this game was going to be complicated but to my surprise the rules were simple and clear but the gameplay is deep and complex, which makes for a worthwhile and fun game. The action point system is the best I have encountered thus far. Even with the myriad of choices to do it doesn't seem to reach the "Analysis Paralysis" level that other games may warrant. There can be lots of player interactions as well as other players try and steal away points and treasures from each other. Highly recommended!

RATING: 8.5
Nem mai darab Kramer-Kiesling páros SdJ díjas játéka, de még ma is megállja a helyét a könnyebb stratégiai játékok között.

A játékosok egy-egy régészekből álló csapattal játszanak (1 vezetőt és 18 régészt fognak mozgatni a játék során) és fedezik fel a maják ősi városát, Tikalt. A cél minél több kincset gyűjteni és templomot feltárni. A zsákmányon azonban nem osztozhatnak a csapatok, az erősebb (többségben lévő) viszi a maják rejtett aranyát.

Újrajátszhatósága magas a moduláris tábla miatt, a szabályzata pár perc alatt tanulható. Minden körben 10 akcióponttal gazdálkodhatunk, miután letettük az új területlapkát (ha az vulkán, akkor értékelő kör következik, amiből egyébként 3 van a játék közben, a játék végi értékelésen kívül). Akciók:
- embereink táborba helyezése: 1 pont / régész
- régész mozgatása: 1 pont / kőtábla
- templom feltárása: 2 pont / szint
- kincs kiásása: 3 pont / kincs
- kincs cseréje másik játékossal: 3 pont / kincs
- templom őrzése (csak ha többségben vagyunk az adott mezőn): 5 pont
- tábor építése: 5 pont

Kb. ennyi, persze van néhány finomság még benne, de tényleg gyorsan megtanulható, illetve nagyon jók a játékosok segédtáblái, amiken néhány piktogram a teljes szabálykönyvet leképezi.

Családi és gamer játéknak is kiváló (van profi szabályváltozata is, amiben licitálás megy a területlapkákért, illetve kicsit más a pontozás is).

Egyik kedvenc.

Update (2018.02.22.): eladtam, de csak mert tervben van az új kiadás beszerzése, addig meg jó lesz a SpielByWeb is.

RATING: 8.5
Second hand

We bought this one quite recently second hand because we heard a lot of good things about it. Tikal is a nice mixture of playing tiles (cfr Carcassonne), area control (cfr El Grande) and action points (cfr Pandemic). These three combined, make it a real classic board game.

The rules are relatively easy. You need some luck with the tile drawing (at least in the basic game) and the treasure hunting but it is definitely a strategic game where you need to concur for the best temples in the rain forest! A joy to play, also with 2 players but it is a little bit too long especially with more players.

RATING: 7
As I've played more Kramer and Kiesling games, I've gotten to the point that I'm not the biggest fan of their action point-driven system that they usually utilize. However, I think Tikal is the best implementation of that system. The 10 points you get from round to round feels like a lot, and it can seem overwhelming at times, but I really like it. You have just enough freedom to do what you want, but you still have to really puzzle out your turn. Definitely avoid this with AP prone players though. The game already runs a touch too long for what it is, but I still think it holds up really well. I'm not a fan of the micro-managed stack of tiles though

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The finest achievement by Kramer & Kiesling? The gameplay is deceivingly simple - use action points to move explorers, build temples and claim treasures. But there is a logistical challenge to decide when and where to add explorers. And there is a competitive challenge to decide which temples to claim majority around and which to give up to your opponents. And there is even a cooperative challenge to decide which temples to extend and which treasures to trade without giving away benefits to your opponents.

I particularly like how the scoring round allows each player to act before scoring, making your own positioning more important than timing dependent on other players' actions. Some people think AP may be an issue but I believe that intuition to predict where the best scoring opportunities will be is more important than raw optimization. The modular board and the dynamic gameplay makes every new game feel fresh.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

First impressions: This may be a little too mean for me. I like the tile placement and set collection elements, and the look of the game, and I even like the action point system. But it feels like the battles for control for the temples just sort of takes over. Now, that is mitigated somewhat since scoring happens on different turns for each player, so in theory both players can score for any given temple, but in practice it's easy for one player to do all the work and the other player to get the points. It's a problem a lot of worker placement games have, but I just don't care for that element so much.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.9
Tikal provides a range of interesting decisions and player interactions. Adherence to theme is excellent which is upheld by quality components and striking artwork. The game can become quite intense with 4 players with everyone battling for control of the best scoring positions. Scoring rounds can slow to a crawl due to analysis paralysis as there can be considered an "optimum solution" in terms of maximum point potential. Each game can play out quite differently to the last but replay-ability could have been further expanded had a third means of scoring points been incorporated. Despite these small misgivings, Tikal is great fun and it is most enjoyable to watch your expedition workers explore the jungle and fight for the greatest discoveries.
