Inspires both a feeling of cooperative exploration as each new tile is drawn and a sense of competition as players position said tiles to manipulate roads and muscle in on pyramids for points. The 10 Action Points per player per round can indeed result in AP among some, but with decisive players the game plays very smoothly and more than makes up for thinking time spent in tactical possibilities.

RATING: 5
I like Tikal's board and hexes, but the temples are fiddly and the choice of player colors are downright ugly. 

Gameplay really shifts as components wind down. As temple upgrades and treasures vanish the area control aspect of the game becomes top priority.

Scoring rounds are a bitch in 2-player, in a 4-player game they can only be worse. Players each get a chance to outdo one another before scoring. The count-up can be difficult due to the sporadic placement of workers.

I honestly don't know if I like Tikal. It is designed well enough, but I don't think I like its gameplay.

RATING: 8
In Tikal you are trying to discover different areas of the board and build your pyramids onto the hexes while also digging for treasure.  Every turn you have 9 action points to use, and a bunch of different actions to choose from with varying degrees of AP costs.

The system is tight and lends itself to some analysis paralysis, but it is a great game that is a joy to play.

RATING: 7
Justifiably a classic, and somehow nothing has since been designed which plays quite like Tikal. The theme seems silly at first, but somehow works with the mechanics, and the much-heralded Action Point system is still fresh and doesn't take as much time to execute as one might imagine.

The auction variant is more of a mildly-interesting variant than an advanced game. I see nothing wrong with sticking to the basic version.

RATING: 7
A mainstream Euro: more of an intellectual exercise rather than being a game played for fun; theme is rather thin, though the superb art and components help give the theme more weight than it would otherwise have; competition is for scoring the most points rather than for eliminating competitors; rules not terribly difficult to grasp, but a fair amount of strategy and decisions to be made throughout. The scoring track goes around the border of the game board. I like the game for what it is. I would not turn down a game, but mainly, I'd have to be in the mood for this kind of Euro game to want to play at a particular time. That is, it's rather dry. Like many others, I rated it somewhat above "Mexica"--in my case, a half notch. It'sa  very good design, but I'm more interested in civilization-themed games about Mesoamerica. Traded in spring of 2008.

RATING: 7.5
心得:
基本規則的翻版塊隨機性有點高
不過規則算是好教易懂
可能要玩競標規則會比較有策略性
規則:
1.放版塊時要放在相連能到達才行，兩塊相鄰只要其中一塊有石階就可通過
2.放人到任何一個帳篷或帳篷移動都一個行動點
3.大頭隊長代表三個人
4.探索神廟每一個探險員能探索一層，同個神廟最多一回合兩層
5.挖掘寶藏一個探險員只能一回挖掘一次，一個人最多一回合一個寶藏窟挖兩次，寶藏有8種
6.交換不能拒絕，但成對以上不能被換走
7.建帳篷不需要有人，只要是空地或是挖完的寶藏窟，一塊地只能有一個帳篷
8.成為神廟首位，必須是該塊最多人的，一個上廟，該區其他該玩家的人移出遊戲，一場最多兩次
9.抽到火山版塊，該玩家10AP棟完記分，之後輪流，全部輪完，在放火山10AP，神廟平手都沒分
10.最後一塊放完10AP動完，在下一家開始進行記分輪

RATING: 8
It’s not just about min/maxing your action points to probable victory points, but deciding where to set up so as to take points from the correct person. Or at least make it costly for them to score. If you spread yourself too thin, everyone’s going to pile into your temples. Therefore consolidate workers on a few big temples to discourage theft. Then try and steal some lower ranked temples which aren’t as guarded. There are good decisions every turn, combining strategic setup and tactics. It doesn’t rate higher due to downtime and the ease with which a player can be taken out of the game through accidental collusion, though usually induced by poor setup / defense admittedly. Still, there's plenty of meat to keep the grey cells turning.

RATING: 6
Love this game - best of the 3 in series (Tikal, Java, Mexica). Lots of interesting strategic and tactical choices to be made - rewards moves that "project power" into a space, without actually moving there. Random element is refreshing, and serves to limit the best player from devouring the others. Most fun had playing this game in a long time (since Settlers was brand new for us).

12/20 Tough to get this to the table, others have not enjoyed this as much as me.

Oct 2014: I played a number of games of this on iOS, and the AI is zero trouble. Against good human opponents, the game can get VERY nasty - and seems to devolve immediately down to a key pyramid that can be upgrade to a 8-10 and "sat-upon" by as few workers as possible for the rest of the game (though the 5-point action where you can cap them). I am concerned that I may have explored the essential game space here, and that the game may be mostly about player-induced randomness, and falling victim or not to being hosed by someone because of where you fall in the turn order. I am lowering my rating of the game, but I do recommend others try this if you have the game-time available, and have a smallish collection. There are a LOT of other good titles out today (2014) that are quite fun, less prone to AP (this one is notorious for it due to it's action-point design). Give Agricola a try for example, I think there is more fun to be had there.

Great graphic design, solid game, but I think after 30+ plays (including my iOS excursions) this one is done.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A fun strategy game about managing possibilities, balancing power, and building a shared board. Needs to be played with four people, or two if playing Mini-Tikal. The game is gorgeous as players place tiles onto the board and develop a unique landscape every time.

Fights over temple control can be exceptionally tense and very interesting decisions can be made about sacrificing your workers to secure a contested site. It's an eye-opener to see just how many different decisions you can make, and it's mentally stimulating to see how you can make the best of the board you have at the beginning of each turn.

The auction variant is a must if you dislike random chance impacting gameplay, and Mini-Tikal is excellent for those with less time or who desire a more intensely and directly competitive experience.

Some randomness still impacts gameplay with treasure draws, and it is possible for one player to entrench/turtle themselves into an unassailable position if left alone during a couple critical turns. 

Not recommended for players who suffer from analysis paralysis, you have a lot of options each turn. I view this a both a strength and a weakness.

Downtime can also be an issue because there's nothing at all you can do during another player's turn since the entire board can change before it's your turn again. The auction variant mitigates the downtime somewhat with the auction mechanic that allows you to stay engaged, but once you've won an auction you just have to sit back and watch the board change dramatically each turn.

Needs more tries, with actual opponents.  Does feel like it'd be simple enough for 7-8 year olds, which is a plus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
An interesting area control game that suffers from unintentional kingmaking. If someone remains unopposed for temple scoring they will win the game. Unfortunately this game requires that you attack the leader after the first scoring round or the outcome will be decided for you. The problem with that is if only one player does this they usually cede the game to the person in second place. I see no way of correcting this problem with the way the scoring round system is designed. Regardless, AP prone gamers absolutely destroy any desire you have to play this game again, which is a shame because the game can be enjoyabe otherwise.

Pros:
* Gorgeous production value.
* Easy to learn, easy to teach.
* Player aid is surprisingly useful.
* Well designed box insert.

Cons:
* -2 for AP-prone gamers grinding this to a halt and sucking all the fun out of the game.
* How the scoring round works could have been clearer.
* Box cardboard could have been thicker.
* Unintentional kingmaking.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I own the IOS version of it. It was a steal since it was on sale for $1. The first game with the easiest AI has shown me that this is a very good game. I can see this game being bugged down by AP players so I will happily play this game on the Ipad.

Tikal is a great game, and provides an interesting environment of exploration mixed with area of control.  The action point mechanism is a new one for me as well.  I like to play this one a lot, but it's best played with your more anlytical friends.  The APs can cause a bit of delay while players decide what to do, but it's negligable - unless you have one of those players who insists on analyzing everything before finishes he his move. RATING: 8
I really enjoy the explorer theme. This is a nice area control mechanic game that gives room for multiple players to score for the same regions. The way it works, is when you enter a scoring round, each player essentially takes 1 free turn and then scores, so each player has a chance to move their units around.

It's a really interesting mechanic I wish I saw more often. You can also build your areas of the board higher making them worth more, and hopefully causing them to be more contested if the other players are paying attention.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A large box game in every sense of the term. Tikal is a resource management game (the 10 action points used every turn are the resources) set in the jungles of Tikal.

The theme and mechanics integrate quite well and visually, the game is very appealing - the forest getting cleared with every turn, the growing size of the temples as more of it is uncovered, the different explorer teams having their own base camps etc.

The game box is also very well designed and the insert has a specific place for every component. The only downside is that the game can be a pain if a player has AP and ruminates endlessly over the most optimal play possible. The base version in which the tiles are not auctioned runs normally to about 90 minutes but the tile auction version, while reducing luck, tends to add a good 45-60 mins to the regular game duration.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Update: Four player games are much more interesting. Not sure if the artifacts are worthwhile.

I have only played two player, but believe it is broken for two.  There are three possible strategies in the game: Either build up temples, search for treasure, or a comination of both.  In a two player game, these choices are reduced mere randomness of the tiles/treasures drawn due to the zero-sum nature of the game (game theory).  For example: If player one chooses to search for treasure, automatically there is no incentive for player two to do the same as any single treasure drawn can be swapped by player one to make a better set.  In this case, player two will only be helping player one's vp total.  Therefore, player two will have to choose only to build temples and hope that player one will not draw good combinations of treasures until the end of the game.  At this point, the game has become rote and the players might as well play rock-paper-scissors (though r-p-s has more meta gaming and may be a better game than Tikal).

My rating reflects the two player "game".

However, I have not played a 3-4 player game of this and my opinions may change with that experience.

RATING: 5
A fascinating game in theory.  I only played once but can see that the strategy is very subtle and worth exploring.  The bits are fantastic, even though the theme is not as strong as some people say it is (perhaps because we were playing the auction variant).  The only problem turns out to be a major problem--it's a very very slow game.  Unfortunately, it's not the kind of game that's fun unless you are really trying your best, and trying your best means spending a lot of time thinking.  Like playing scrabble, every additional minute of thought is bound to get you extra points, making it a painful game as you wait for everyone else to move.  Lots of points for originality and interest, minus a lot of points for taking WAAY too long.  

As an alternative, I might suggest Hansa, which has the same sort of logistical decisions to be made, but is much quicker (and lighter).

RATING: 7
A lot of fun to play, I only have a few minor issues with this game.
1. )The standard game suffers a bit from the chance introduced by random tile draws. Two rounds of bad (or good) draws can completely tip the game in one player's favor.
2.) The auction variant eliminates some of the chance but it also tips the game in favor of the lead player (especially late in the game). Bidding is done with victory points, so players with even a modest lead have a heavy advantage in tile auctions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Over the past year, so many people whose opinion that I respect have begun to rave about the game again that I decided that I needed to give it another chance - but using the auctions.  We did, and I'm sold.  
Let's just say that I have seen that this game has much more depth and replayablity than I was giving it credit for.  I think that there were 2 factors that have convinced me to put Tikal back into my playing rotation.  
1)  The auction really does add some interesting elements.  Drawing and placing a tile doesn't allow for much long term planning and really has the effect of minimizing strategic decisions, so without the auction the game becomes more tactical and more luck driven.
2)  I don't believe that I had ever played the game with people that I would consider "serious" gamers.  This definitely had an effect on the "feel" of the game.  When played seriously, you have to be constantly on the lookout for ways to maximize your points, while trying to minimize the help that you give to your opponents.  With the wrong people Tikal could definitely fall into analysis paralysis, but the 3 of us kept things moving along fairly well (I think that I probably held things up the most, and I think that was mostly because I was learning the auction part of the game...)

RATING: 9
:thumbsup: Very good usage of the action point mechanism. Almost every turn you're thinking: [i]"Damn, if only I'd haved one more action point to pull off everything I want to do..."[/i]
:thumbsup: It has a fun learning curve, with great replayability. Every time I play, I feel I've 'upped my game'. Meanwhile, the game itself keeps balance between experienced and new players with an element of controlled randomness due to the tile draws: you have a rough idea of when a type of tile can come up, but you never know exactly when it will be drawn (e.g. volcano tiles that start a scoring round, or the fact that temples closer to the bottom of the stack have higher point values while treasure spots contain more treasure tokens at the top of the deck than at the bottom).
:thumbsup: The Ravensburger edition has a delightfully rustic appearance. I recommend you put on one of the many [url=https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-IAfg_Iy7n8]rainforest YouTube-videos[/url] on in the background, to increase your immersion.

RATING: 9
Great components (though it really needs some glass beads or something to use for action points), and a good meaty game to go with them. It seems a bit multiplayer solitaire with just two people, and even with three people one player can get away with having their own little fiefdom. I suspect that four players might be the best number for Tikal, though you'd need to pick them carefully to avoid AP. And it's best with the auction variant (it can be a bit of a luckfest otherwise) IF you have quick thinking players.

Feels a little preprogrammed to me compared with other Kramer games. The best move is often obvious, because movement costs reduce your possibilites down to a couple of options. It's a solid game, but I prefer Maharaja to scratch the same itch. I'd recommend Tikal for people who are looking to move on from gateway games into something slightly meatier. It's about the same weight as Power Grid.

Update: bumped to an 8 after a few more plays. Very enjoyable.

Update: still great, still want to play more. A better design than Maharaja, in retrospect. Promoted to 9.

RATING: 7
The father of the "action point" system.  Players get 10 points to spend each turn doing various actions, each with their own cost.  The goal is to accumulate points by exploring a jungle, collecting/stealing matching treasures, and uncovering temples.  There are multiple ways to get points and focusing on a few is better than trying to get them all.  Given there are only a few scoring rounds, which are semi-random, and the quick, easiness of play, this makes a great gateway game.  Also a fantastic game for 2 players.

RATING: 7.5

UPDATE: Increasing rating to 7.5 The game plays much better with people who can make faster decisions and plan their moves while other people are taking turns. I find a good strategy is to stake out your own area of the board and build it up, trying to get the other players to nip at each other's heels instead of yours.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In Tikal the decision space starts out narrow and widens as you add more workers and tiles to explore until it reaches its zenith in the end game. Java's decision space starts out as wide or wider than Tikal's final state and remains pretty much constant throughout due to its vertical placement options. And, finally, Mexica's decision space starts out wide open and slowly narrows as more and more of the board becomes sectioned off by canals and locked down into districts. Looking at the series in this way, you can begin to see why people may suggest that Java would be the most AP prone of the lot: in Tikal, AP potential appears to rise as the number of options available rises due to increases in the number of workers and cleared jungle tiles; in Java, it starts high and remains fairly constant from beginning to end; and, in Mexica, it seems to slowly decrease, but never disappear, as the game wears on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I will not play this with analysis-paralysis types!!!! That said, I especially enjoy Tikal as a two player game (less downtime, more feeling of head-to-head competition). It plays in about an hour, and provides for a pretty intense experience. I like how Tikal manages to blend tactics with strategy. Over the course of a game, you have a rough idea of when scoring is going to occur (basic version), and so are able to plan ahead to some degree. You also have the option to build up to two additional base camps, which increase your mobility in different regions of the board. Tile placement is also rather strategic, and allows you to shape the board throughout the game to your advantage, or your opponents' detriment. The use of action points forces you to make more tactical decisions, and the scoring rounds themselves are an exercise in analytical tactics. "Analytical" - that's a word that is very appropriate to this game. Every turn, you must analyze which use of your available action points (10 each turn) give you the most reward - both for the next scoring round, and for the game overall. So the game requires analysis, and it doesn't really evoke the passions as a result. I don't get excited about Tikal the way I do about some other games, but I'm up for a game most of the time. Enjoyable.

Update: I am frankly surprised that I like this game as much as I do. I wouldn't classify it as an especially rich game, and I don't spend any time thinking about how I would play differently once a game is over. Neither do I anticipate playing it with the thrill accompanying, say, Union Pacific or Struggle of Empires. But I'm usually willing to play it, and enjoy doing so. Perhaps it is a kind of intellectual exercise that simply scratches an itch? More players makes the game more interesting to me, and increases the role of strategy versus tactics (which I like!), but the downtime is nearly *unbearable*. And with more players in the game, your positional options are further limited, giving you less thinking to do during all that tedious waiting. With two players, the game isn't quite as interesting, but it plays much, much faster. Tikal is very hard for me to rate objectively in terms of its excellence as a game, but it's easy for me to determine that it's something I definitely need to be "in the mood" to play.

Final update (& verdict): I really have a love-hate relationship with Tikal. Sometimes, major arm twisting is required for me to play, because it simply feels like a very tedious logical exercise of navigating a too-dense decision tree. But there isn't any *fun* in Tikal's brain-burniness. I give it a 4. Other times, it does kind of "scratch the itch" - but even then, it isn't loads of fun. It simply satisfies the part of me that enjoys this kind of intellectual exercise. I give it a 6. In the end, I'm splitting the difference. Tikal is a 5 for me.

Absolute final update (seriously!): Tikal, like El Grande, suffers (from my perspective) in that it fails to reward excellent play in the late game. Contrast with Tigris and Euphrates where a mistake on player A's part provides an opening for player B to catch up and win, or with Puerto Rico where you can trail for most of the game, but where your wharf-buying strategy can pay off in the late game. In Tikal, unfortunately, brilliant plays in the late game will avail you very little. So long as the leader plays "competently", her early lead will give her the game. Tikal probably has a greater probability (than, say El Grande) of allowing the trailers to take a leader down - but not by much. This bleeds a lot of the tension out of the game, and for me anyway, diminishes the fun.

RATING: 7.5
Wow, I'm a retard.  When I first played this it was with a very slow player.  At the time this came out I was a newbie to the whole Euro thing, and felt a little overwhelmed by the min/maxing, irritated at the downtime and reflexively felt guilty if my turns took too long.  At the same time it had a random element that clearly benefited those that drew better tiles, so all the hard work I would put in it seemed secondary to drawing treasure tiles or big temples.  I was frustrated by the system and table courtesy. What I was looking for was a heavier Euro game with a smoother transition between players, less downtime, while being less luck dependent.  In time I found those games.

Now, revisiting it, I see that it still has the same "problems". Now, though, I've found players that won't take forever with this, and I've learned to play this kind of game fairly quick, so the luck of a draw remains the last primary nuisance.  I have yet to play this with the auction variant, which should solve my issue.  

That said, even playing the base game as is, I like it now.  In fact, I really like it.  It is quite a turn around of opinion.  This still means that the game has the potential to go south with the wrong people, but the same can certainly be said of the other games in this series, and in fact with most games in general.  

Still, despite the bump on the charts it is my least favorite of the Mask Series.  That may change with the auctions, but I doubt it.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A great game of exploration. Looks beautiful, too!

Players have 10 action points. Each turn, you first take the top hex tile (arranged in groups A, B, ... G; random order within groups) and place it on the board connected to what's already there (board starts with 3 hexes already revealed). Connections are by stone markers on the hex-sides which form paths -- there must be a path connecting every new tile to a previously placed area. Tiles show a variety of things (temples of various levels; collections of 3-4 treasures; empty space; volcanos --> scoring round). Then, you spend your 10 AP to do various things (bring workers into play, move workers around, unearth more temple levels, take treasure from board, trade treasure with other player, etc.). If the volcano was drawn, it's not played immediately; instead, you take your normal turn, then score (score for temples you control [= more workers than anyone else], 1pt/temple level; and for your jewelry collection. Everyone else does the same. THEN, you resume the game: place the volcano and take your turn. There are 3 volcano/scoring rounds during the game: 1 each in the B, D, and F hex groups. At the end of the game, there's another scoring round. Most points wins!

The advanced variant is better: instead of just drawing from the top of the hex pile, at the start of each round you reveal as many tiles as there are players. Then, players auction the right to choose which tile to take, and take their turn. Repeat each round. This version takes away the luck of the tile draw, but does take a bit longer to play.

RATING: N/A
Ganador del Spiel des Jahres 1999
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Tikal is an achievement in board gaming. The freedom of the action point system in combination with strategic planning for the endgame/scoring rounds and tactical decision making each turn is brilliant. Generally, luck does not play a huge factor in the game since you really have control over the majority of your turn. There is an amazing sense of progression as the tiles are placed each turn, and every move a player makes impacts the future game. The really cool theme of exploring temples in the jungles of Mexico may be a hard sell for some folks, but in my opinion it just makes sense. The criticisms that this game is "AP inducing" are largely unfounded; I would suggest that this game was intended to be played multiple times, as are most strategy games, thereby reducing play time with experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Finally, a game that will allow me to use 10 action points per turn instead of only a couple of simple actions!  And what a beautifully illustrated game it is!  But most importantly, it is fun to play and does a good job of instilling that feeling of anticipation that comes with exploration.  I only have a few minor complaints with this game.  The first is that it can be difficult to catch up if you start to fall behind in points, and it's nearly impossible to make up the lost ground if a player is down more than a handful of points after the second scoring round (especially in a two-player game).  Secondly, with the only two scoring possibilities coming from owning treasures or uncovering temples, it sometimes feels like the options are either very limited or fairly obvious.  It would be nice to have a couple more ways to gain points (or how about some dangers lurking in the jungle, waiting to catch an unsuspecting explorer?).  To make things slightly more interesting, I give the treasures different values: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/file/info/32356.  Thirdly, it's a bit of a nasty game, with players constantly trying to steal each others' highest-scoring temples and treasures.  But that being said, it's still a very enjoyable and competitive game of exploration.  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 3/5  Total: 7/10

RATING: 7
Area control game with an exploration theme. The mechanism is driven by action points and each player has to allocate their points as each turn a new part of the board is added, thereby uncovering new parts of the temples and jungle. The action points allow you to perform different functions such as moving members of your expedition, uncovering extra layers of the temples, discovering treasures etc. In order to gain victory points you need to have overall control of different temples and collect sets of treasures. The scoring system is well balanced and serves to keep games tight. It's difficult to fault Tikal as an example of area control gameplay, in fact it achieves its aims very well, it's just that I always seem to find games like this slightly detached from their theme resulting in a somewhat abstract exercise.

RATING: 7.5
Tikal is a beautiful looking game, based on a great premise, with sound mechanics, clear easy to learn rules and excellent player interaction.  However, after one playing (so I could be wrong), it seems that it becomes bogged down during the closing stages.  Movement, excavation, treasure hunting (all the main elements to the game) become more and more difficult to the point where some spaces become untouched.  This, of course, might have been just a symptom of the particular session we were playing, but it does seem that the game itself is designed to create this situation.  I just wonder why.  Perhaps experienced players become better at maximising the limited opportunities of the end game.  Only time will tell.

RATING: 8.3

Update: This is a classic K&K design; everything just works wonderfully and I'm constantly impressed how often players are one AP short of what they want to do. Just a brilliant piece of design.

RATING: 10
This is my favorite game. I like how this game combines area control with tile placement. I also like the points mostly come from excavating and controlling temples, and treasures are usually secondary. The scoring is cool because you never know exactly when your position will be scored. As an archaeologist, I feel this game is more faithful to the profession than many other games. I like how Mayan numerals are on the excavation tiles. Excavation seems to be the main points scorer while the stereotypical treasure hunting is secondary. I would prefer treasure to be nonexistent in an archaeology game, but everyone thinks about Indiana Jones when it comes to archaeology.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The first action point allowance game I played. I find the jungle exploration theme enjoyable and the mechanics easy to grasp, although strategies took several plays to begin to learn and refine. One of the things I really appreciate about this game is that it is simple to learn and easily approachable, yet provides many layers of strategic depth. In addition, replayability is extremely high due to the tile-laying aspect.

The tile-auction variant is a nice touch, as it allows you to remove the element of luck in the form of the blind tile draw found in the base game. I've found player aids in the form of action point tracking sheets are a must and can be found in the "Files" section of the game home page. Free online play is available at www.SpielByWeb.com for those wanting a better feel for the game.

RATING: 9
This game is excellent. I love that you start off with this little army and over the game that army travels around and sets up strongholds. It's all the good parts of area control with out any of the bad parts (e.g. Risk-style battles that take forever and don't move the front). Straightforward to teach. Only have two very minor quibbles which are that the volcano scoring round is a bit confusing, and the "paths" on the tiles make it look like those are blockades and you're supposed to go around through the clearings, which is the exact opposite of what the designer meant. Not a big deal. The best part is how holding onto those high value pyramids as the scoring round gets closer is super stressful, strategic, and fun, and the way the scoring works means that two players can score for the same tile, which makes it less cut-throat, meaning this is one of the few area control games I can imagine playing light-heartedly with family. And even still, it doesn't lose that strategic edge. One of my top games, and my favorite area control.

Update:  Rating stays the same.  I think the Heavy Gateway still apllies but it might be a bit too much for many.  I think that Puerto Rico still makes a better heavy gateway.  Not because it is necessarily lighter than Tikal, but because it feels lighter when first introduced.

I highly recommend the short version posted in the files section and I even more highly recommend the removalof certain tiles for 2 and 3 player games.  In order to find the tiles for removal you'll have to search through the forums and possibly cached files on the internet.  I don't precisely remember how I got the rules

Update: Played this again recently after a long layoff. The rating stays the same as I enjoyed my plays. I can see why games with lots of action points allowed per turn are not really designed anymore as it does create a much longer playtime, but one's turns can be very rewarding too. 



Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

RATING: 6
Update: Java is superior.

*

Beautiful. Tile placement pleasing with the creation of both barriers & Action Point costs for routes so the photographs of the game in progress belies the complex incentive-paths builds. The worker maneuvers it creates with a relatively simple looking large hex arrangement is just right to make each turn rife with choices just the other side of calculable due to the randomness of the tile placement & the other's behavior in response to the cost-benefit riddle of treasures vs board development.  

Tikal has a fun undertow rather than a stressful one of churn, & while the 2-player lasts too long (it's worth experimenting with shortening by removing some tiles) I felt no real downtime or distress as it was interesting to watch each other player solve the riddles presented them as they accept & provoke new proffers. 

I do understand some reporting the game overstays & can cause AP like an abstract. This doesn't bother me at all, I like it when a positional situation causes some mulling over the board, & familiarity of the game seems the simple antidote.

Played 1st time with M. Look forward to Java soon & comparing.

RATING: 7
Tikal is a game of exploration within the Central American jungles in search of lost temples and the treasures within. Players send their team of explorers into the jungle, exposing more and more of the terrain. Along the way, you find temples that require further uncovering and treasures. Players attempt to score points for occupying temples and holding onto treasure.

RATING: 9
My fourth favorite game.

The only thing I don't like is ping-ponging 2 workers between two temples one step away and increasing both temples levels by two every turn.

Should be:
1) Any given temple can only be raised by 2 levels on a player's turn.
2) One worker is needed per leveling.
3) A worker leveling a temple cannot level another temple in the same turn, but may move before and after he levels.

RATING: 9
I have had my eye on this game for months, and bought it at Origins 04.  You really have some interesting decisions to make when playing this game.  When I was first reading about the game, I didn't really understand how effectively you can block people in this game.  Sure, it is nearly impossible to block a loan explorer, but a loan explorer is rarely significant.  I love the gambling element about digging for treasure and placing guards.  Do I guard early, or wait until I have a more valuable temple, but have to lose alot of explorers to guard it?  There is plenty of interaction, but it rarely feels like screwage.  Its just a nice balance.  Our group is prone to AP, but I am a patient person, and patience is its own reward in this fascinating game.

RATING: N/A
Only played 2 player.
I was scared of playing this game for ages because of its reputation for inducing analysis paralysis, something I can be prone to, but it's really not that bad.

The 'mini Tikal' variant is great for learning the rules, which are refreshingly simple, then we went on to the full game. Very enjoyable. It's 'thinky' but not too much so. Luck in the form of tile draws takes the pressure off nicely for me, allowing a focus on tactical play.

It really feels like you're playing an 'elderly statesman' of a game; classy and streamlined.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
The Good: The action-point system really gives the brain a workout...

The Bad: ...when you are the active player. There's not much to do between turns. Oh, I try to draw up plans for my next turn. But the tile-draws keep things fluid enough that you hit a point of diminishing returns pretty quickly.

The Ugly: The action-point system almost demands some analysis-paralysis during scoring rounds -- which can really stretch that downtime. 

Still, it's really pretty, mostly fun, and there are a lot of hard decisions to make -- all-in-all a very good game. It's just the kind of game I enjoy more over the interweb (SBW) where time between rounds isn't an issue (unless, natch, someone goes on holiday mid-game).

RATING: 7
The second of the mask trilogy I've played, after [gameid=855].  I enjoyed this one more than Java.  It's not exactly a fast game, but also doesn't seem too overburdened by the problems of analysis paralysis that I hear about it.  The actions are intuitive enough, and there's lots of planning for the future.  Sure, the camp might not be a great strategy now, but it could reap dividends next turn.  And sometimes you're going to have to spend the resources to create a guard.  The theme could be neat, but unfortunately misses as somewhat strangely abstracted in odd ways.  It doesn't see like exploring when the players get to decide where the temples are.  I do like how clusters of the map will be more accessible to certain players, and other areas will be way too far away from any kind of strong point.

RATING: 8
Overall, a much better, meatier, thinkier game than Tikal II. Love everything about it: the element of exploration, thinking about positioning the tiles, messing with other players to make sure they don't get too far ahead, area control, and the auction. Several clever mechanics make the game fair and balanced and there is even a catch-up mechanic in the end game scoring that gives priority to least points amassed in ascending order. I would want to own this but don't have a regular group of 4 non-AP players, plus Eugene and corn already own it. This game can be a killer with AP.

RATING: N/A
Wolfgang Kramer & Michael Kiesling; Tikal is a game of exploration within the Central American jungles in search of lost temples and the treasures within. Players send their team of explorers into the jungle, exposing more and more of the terrain. Along the way you find temples which require further uncovering and treasures. Players attempt to score points for occupying temples and holding onto treasure. Part of the Mask Trilogy (Java and Mexica are the other two).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is interesting, but I can see this being a serious problem for AP-prone players.  I liked playing through a PBEM format, because it eliminated the downtime issues that are problematic in the game.  I'd be interested in playing it face-to-face sometime, but I imagine I would get frustrated, depending on the group playing it.  In the past, my groups have been AP-prone, and I imagine this taking FAR too long with them.

2 plays: 4 players

Rating: 7

-----

AUCTION VARIANT: Good Lord, if the base game doesn't take long enough for you, try the auction variant.  It's frustrating to be at the end of the turn order during the auction, though, since even if everyone else passes, you're in a position to either bid, or go last ... AGAIN.  Going last means getting the crappiest tile to play, and thus reducing your points even further.  So it's an endless cycle, unless you're willing to part with your hard-earned points.

1 play: 4 players

RATING: 4
For what is a deep thinking game I felt like there was too much unmitigated randomness (luck) in the tile draws, especially in the artifacts. Our game had some real winkiness with one guy never getting to place his second tent due to lack of the open tiles and another guy having three full sets of treasure. 

I don't mind luck all that much depending on the game. This one I felt like just had the luck totally out of place. It would be like blindly drawing your Hive tiles. It just felt like a klunky mechanic and made the game unenjoyable to me. (Which is a pity because it did have a lot of other things going for it)

It is a very pretty game but I wish the colors were more standard. I couldn't be blue! (A very minor gripe)

I'm told there is a variant that allows bidding VP's for the tiles, I'd like to try that as I think it would take away most of my frustrations.

RATING: 9
Very good theme and components.  Plays *much* better with the auction version.  The auction version keeps you more involved (since you have to think about what you would have done with each tile, you now care about what your opponent is doing) and makes the downtime unnoticeable.  Also, here's an analysis paralysis avoidance trick:  as you play this game more you develope rules about what to fight for and the conflicts become less frequent(i.e. more negotiated) resulting in a fast game. in particular if a pyramid is 4 moves for you and 2 moves from the other guy let him have it because he can move men there twice as good as you.  Once it becomes clear whose winning then you can resume fighting for them.

RATING: 5
Another one of Kramer's "optimize your turn" games. Reminds me of Goldland in many ways. Despite the theme the game feels cold and clinical. Often there's a feeling of aimlessness. Upgrading temples will only attract other players' explorers, so you just make more explorers yourself, and there isn't much else to do if there aren't any treasure sites to scavenge. So, on a lot of turns you just make explorers, hop them around a bit and wait for a scoring turn or treasure site to arrive. The game looks very cool, but in the end isn't just very much fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really like games such as Tikal which give you many choices to win.  At first it seemed very daunting with the decisions you can make each turn, whether to dig to increase the value of the temple, to excavate the treasures, or to advance your units further to grab the advantage on newly discovered places.  The randomness of the tiles you draw, as well as the strategic placement that will allow you the best access while deterring or even denying your opponents from entering, makes the game even more complex and exciting.  I don't know if it will stand up to multiple plays, but for now I can't wait to get another game going.

RATING: 8
Számomra ez volt az a játék, mely elhitette, hogy egy táblás társasjáték még felnőtt korban is nagyszerű élmény lehet. Azóta szenvedélyes társasjátékgyűjtővé váltam, bár ez nem jelent nagy gyűjteményt. Inkább a különleges darabokat keresem, melyek beindítják a fantáziát, kicsi bennük a szerencse faktor, viszont sok logikát és taktikázást, és stratégiai gondolkodást igényelnek. Esetleg még egyedi játékmechanizmust is. Ja, és ne tartson hosszú órákon át, mert a mindennapi életem annyi időre nem ad lehetőséget.  A Tikál majdnem ilyen. 
(Azt leszámítva, hogy ha ritkán játszik vele az ember, a szabályokat átböngészni sok idő, és a játék ideje sem kevés. Ez az egyedüli ok, ami miatt ritkán kerül elő).

Lenyűgöző az a mechanizmus, ahogyan egy dzsungel sűrűjéből előkerülnek a maja templomok, ráadásul ezek a templomok még térben is növekszenek, ahogyan egyre több szintet ásnak ki belőlük a régészcsapatok. De van itt rejtett kincs és aktív vetélkedés is egymás ellen. Mindez káprázatos rajzokkal övezve.

Nagyjából 17 éve van meg ez a társas, és ma is ugyanolyan kreatív, komplex játékélménynek találom, mint akkor, amikor megvettem.

RATING: 7
Ten action points makes for too large a decision tree.

In spite of these complaints it's a pretty good game, one I'd be happy to play again.
